<div id="review_47898869" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47898869" />
    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/159602-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442450689p2/159602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47898869?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/159602-jamie">Jamie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780375714122" href="/book/show/759823.The_Dying_Animal">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/159602-jamie?shelf=prose-fiction">prose-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer47898869&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47898869" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4286996067044858559">I've always liked Roth and had bought this book as a slightly mashed copy on a bargain table several years ago. I pulled it out to read in anticipation of reviewing the DVD of <i>Elegy</i>, the film adaptation from last year. <i>The Dying Animal</i> is a short novel, and one that I must like in spite of myself, in spite of my often horrified reaction to the selfishness and intensity of the sexual peccadilloes of its narrator. David Kepesh is one of Roth's regular characters, and the man who turned into a gian</span>
  <span id="freeText4286996067044858559" style="display:none">I've always liked Roth and had bought this book as a slightly mashed copy on a bargain table several years ago. I pulled it out to read in anticipation of reviewing the DVD of <i>Elegy</i>, the film adaptation from last year. <i>The Dying Animal</i> is a short novel, and one that I must like in spite of myself, in spite of my often horrified reaction to the selfishness and intensity of the sexual peccadilloes of its narrator. David Kepesh is one of Roth's regular characters, and the man who turned into a giant tit in the appropriately titled <i>The Breast</i>, a parody of Kafka and one of the funniest things I've ever read. (Inside joke in this book being an admiration of a hand-written Kafka manuscript binding Kepesh with his fetish object.) <br /><br />Kepesh isn't so much an unreliable narrator as one that is both myopic and didactic regarding the myopia. His story here is one of obsession regarding his long-term existence as an unapologetic, often evangelical libertine, and the crystallization of that lifestyle in one woman, the 24-year-old Consuela Castillo, and her rather large chest. The 62-year-old Kepesh becomes so overtaken by his desire for her, it begins to threaten the cavalier lifestyle he has so meticulously constructed.<br /><br />What makes <i>The Dying Animal</i> slightly sickening to read is Kepesh's effusive recounting of his pleasures with Consuela. Talk about the male gaze, this book is one long, creepy stare! What makes me feel pangs of guilt, though, is how mesmerizing it can be, almost like Kepesh is hypnotizing me. Roth writes the book in long paragraphs, no dialogue breaks, just strings of traded conversation, and it compels the reader to move quickly through the novel, to feverishly go from page to page.<br /><br />The "sex and death" coupling is nothing new, but Roth has inextricably entwined them here in this narrative. To borrow a metaphor he uses in the book--though he relates it as the trade-off of subservience and domination that is part of any sexual encounter--it is as if he has braided the two, creating one criss-cross strand where once there was a pair (and, admittedly, a pair that began from the same source, as the hair would come from the same head). Since <i>The Dying Animal</i> begins with so much about youth and carnality, the switch to ruminations on the aging process and mortality is surprising. Roth even creates a counterpoint to Kepesh's remorseless pursuit of Consuela by introducing us to David's estranged son, who so hates how his father lives, he thoroughly demands the opposite of himself even when it causes him terrible misery. <br /><br />I think Roth is aware that there for all his belief in his own powers of observation, Kepesh is ironically unaware that the story he intends to share about Consuela is never about anyone but himself. Even in the emotional climax, where the knowledge of death prematurely comes to the young, he can't see the frightened heart beating beneath those great big breasts, he only sees the loss of his own desire. Or thinks he does, because it is also never more evident how out of control of his own life he is. I suppose this be <b>a spoiler</b> of sorts--so beware!--but there is a profound narrative switch on the very last page that will take some time to chew on and digest. Throughout the book, it has been made clear that Kepesh's monologue is meant to be a literal one, that he is speaking to someone directly, some invisible witness, a stand-in for the reader. In the last lines, not only does the witness speak up, but for the first time, Roth relates the call-and-response between him and Kepesh as separately rendered paragraphs (though the witness in quotes, Kepesh still not). It's a dramatic change, a last-chance grabbing at something that could be slipping away. Are we implicated now as the confidante, having read all the way to this end? Is Kepesh's obsession now our own?<br /><br />And how the hell can the movie of this exist without either removing some of Kepesh’s beastly opinions or risk the entire audience thinking Ben Kingsley is the biggest slimeball to ever grace the silver screen? Because how can one not be more sympathetic to Penelope Cruz--particularly since in a film we will see her as something more whole than the idealized creature presented through David Kepesh’s eyes? Questions I will have to answer in another review.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4286996067044858559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText14218077817831888838" style="display:none">You see that painting of a nude woman? That's an Amedeo Modigliani painting, a character in this book sends a post card with that nude printed on it. And I, like some naive coed horny for culture, am always impressed with Professor Roth's little references. Here we have Thomas Morton, the early American colonist you didn't learn about in middle school, fornicating with Indian women and upsetting the Puritans at his settlement, aptly named "Merry Mount".<br /><br />It is funny to note the judgmental reviews on this website concerning this book. Roth always understands his critics, and anticipates their disgust in his pages. That doesn't make him any less devious, but it does make him more funny.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1397989911" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer7035652662777808565">"I had asked her if she would take off her clothes and let me look at her while I played the Mozart Sonata in C Minor, and she obliged."<br/><br/>I'm not sure but I have a feeling any post-<i>American Pastoral</i> era Philip Roth borders on self-parody. For sure, <i>The Dying Animal</i> doesn't just border on self-parody, it crosses the border, marries someone for full citizenship, and establishes a thriving quilt business in it. You couldn't do better if you won a round of "Philip Roth Stereotype Bingo." The old, whit</span>
  <span id="freeText7035652662777808565" style="display:none">"I had asked her if she would take off her clothes and let me look at her while I played the Mozart Sonata in C Minor, and she obliged."<br /><br />I'm not sure but I have a feeling any post-<i>American Pastoral</i> era Philip Roth borders on self-parody. For sure, <i>The Dying Animal</i> doesn't just border on self-parody, it crosses the border, marries someone for full citizenship, and establishes a thriving quilt business in it. You couldn't do better if you won a round of "Philip Roth Stereotype Bingo." The old, white, 70-year-old professor of culture living in New York, sleeping with his 20-year-old students, talking about jizz and power, being utterly crass while referencing Schubert but also kind of holding that knowledge of high art over the women he's sleeping with, and, of course, devoting at least 15 pages of this 150-page book to talking about his "crazy bingeing on Consuela's breasts." Dude is obsessed... with these breasts. <br /><br />I still love Philip Roth, and this book had about 5 or 6 genuinely interesting ideas in it. But I assume it's like 80% of his output that makes it hard to be a fan of his, in the face of the great cultural narrative of Philip Roth being a primordial sexist jerko. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7035652662777808565" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/864051-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Snobs who want to say they've read Roth</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33541228" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3181643302008385856">Ok, sort of read it. Got about 30 pages in and could not go any further.<br/>I don't understand why Philip Roth is so popular. He's even been considered for Nobel a few times. Huh? <br/>The most pervasive question I asked myself during the first and very tediously self-righteous 30 pages was whether or not Roth believes himself to be the heir-apparent to Norman Mailer, a comparison I've heard in passing. The primary problem being with that lofty simile is that talking about sex, your prostrate penis and w</span>
  <span id="freeText3181643302008385856" style="display:none">Ok, sort of read it. Got about 30 pages in and could not go any further.<br />I don't understand why Philip Roth is so popular. He's even been considered for Nobel a few times. Huh? <br />The most pervasive question I asked myself during the first and very tediously self-righteous 30 pages was whether or not Roth believes himself to be the heir-apparent to Norman Mailer, a comparison I've heard in passing. The primary problem being with that lofty simile is that talking about sex, your prostrate penis and waning libido like Norman Mailer does not Norman Mailer make. Mailer ostensibly placed his libido in his literature in a near self-depricating fashion where as Roth takes a poorly translated good-ol' boy romantic sentiment and creates misogynistic hyperboles without an attempt at post-modern irony. Speaking of the sexual revolution as successful is to overlook the undergirding of that 'revolution' and blithely disregard the original intent...the sexual 'revolution' was not an intention to ensure that an emaciated post-modern snob could get a good blow job, as is implied if not arguably suggested by Roth.<br />It's all too apparent that Roth either believes he is his own main character David Kepesh, in the flesh (groan), or at the very least he certainly would like to be. Anybody championing Roth's ascention to Norman Mailer's throne as his direct progeny are likely to find themselves in a lonely camp.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3181643302008385856" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tracy Towley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tracy Towley" href="/user/show/1857873-tracy-towley">Tracy Towley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118961904" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2911481292132494414">Right after I finished this book I watched Elegy, which is a movie based on the book. I'd say you could skip the book and go straight to the DVD.<br/><br/>It's not that I didn't enjoy it. I just don't know that I would have enjoyed it if I didn't know as much about Roth's background as I do. Because you see, it was based directly on a situation in his life.<br/><br/>That situation is basically that he's an old man but he still loves the young ladies. He is a professor at a major university, sets his sites on a Cuba</span>
  <span id="freeText2911481292132494414" style="display:none">Right after I finished this book I watched Elegy, which is a movie based on the book. I'd say you could skip the book and go straight to the DVD.<br /><br />It's not that I didn't enjoy it. I just don't know that I would have enjoyed it if I didn't know as much about Roth's background as I do. Because you see, it was based directly on a situation in his life.<br /><br />That situation is basically that he's an old man but he still loves the young ladies. He is a professor at a major university, sets his sites on a Cuban girl in his class and begins sleeping with her. Eventually he does weird things like lick blood off of her legs.<br /><br />The book is a pretty self-indulgent undertaking. It is clearly just him trying to make sense of the affair and an attempt to discern why it affected him so much. I don't think he quite accomplishes that but I did end the book feeling like I'd gotten some useful insight into his own life and how it's affected a few of his other books. <br /><br />Overall : I would not recommend this to anyone who is just starting out with Roth. In fact, I wouldn't even recommend it to a Roth fan who hadn't read both The Professor of Desire and The Breast.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2911481292132494414" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_886047055" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/886047055" />
    <a title="Zachary Karabashliev" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13250875-zachary-karabashliev"><img alt="Zachary Karabashliev" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505302307p2/13250875.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/886047055?book_show_action=true">Mar 19, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zachary Karabashliev" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zachary Karabashliev" href="/user/show/13250875-zachary-karabashliev">Zachary Karabashliev</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2336123752" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2336123752" />
    <a title="Christopher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10350614-christopher"><img alt="Christopher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469809899p2/10350614.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2336123752?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/10350614-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780618135875" href="/book/show/834502.The_Dying_Animal">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2336123752" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17216991274770301910">Before I started reading him -- or at least before I started reading him in earnest, following two books in early twenties which I subsequently forgot almost entirely (my failing, not Roth's) and what I considered a requisite perusal of <i>The Plot Against America</i> in advance of the 2016 election -- this is essentially what I had envisioned as the Plantonic ideal of a Philip Roth novel: an older academic and his younger paramour, prose of great erudition and insight, a smattering of prurient detail,</span>
  <span id="freeText17216991274770301910" style="display:none">Before I started reading him -- or at least before I started reading him in earnest, following two books in early twenties which I subsequently forgot almost entirely (my failing, not Roth's) and what I considered a requisite perusal of <i>The Plot Against America</i> in advance of the 2016 election -- this is essentially what I had envisioned as the Plantonic ideal of a Philip Roth novel: an older academic and his younger paramour, prose of great erudition and insight, a smattering of prurient detail, no shortage of pith, self-aggrandizing and self-excoriating in equal measure.<br /><br />Approaching it after a baker's dozen of his other works, I now of course know that Roth is vastly more than that frequent oversimplification paints him to be. Nonetheless, <i>The Dying Animal</i> feels fairly emblematic, if not of Roth in toto then certainly of a very popular and specific image of him, namely that of the one-time pop-lit enfant terrible, now aged yet still making literary grist of his own licentious history and yearnings. Present here are all of the qualities that Roth admires love (the multidisciplinary allusions, the socio-historical perspicacity, the structural rigor, the metatextual resonances) as are all of those his detractors bemoan (the misogyny, the narcissism, the limited scope), and while I would argue that the former are attributes of the author while the latter are those of his protagonists, neither that distinction nor this novel are likely to change the mind of anyone who is tired of or offended by Roth's oeuvre.<br /><br />For those on Roth's wavelength, however, this is perhaps the best of the Kepesh books, a striking meditation on desire, projection, and mortality, no less affecting for its brevity. (I trust, however, that the younger Roth wouldn't have given Carolyn such short shrift; whether this would have made the book better is fodder for speculation but I believe it could have made it richer.) Yes, Kepesh is an unrepentant libertine, but the exaggeration of his appetites throws his fear of physical decline into sharper relief. Meanwhile, the pat cyclicality of Consuela's coveted bosom as a framing device is as much gallows humor as cosmic irony, and Roth is clearly in on the joke (speaking of framing devices, recall Roth's complaint that many readers mistook him for "a walking prick" when you read the passage relating how bewitchingly Consuela uses her assets to envelop Kepesh's manhood).<br /><br />David Kepesh, of course, originated in Roth's Kafka riff <i>The Breast</i> several decades earlier as a man inexplicably transformed into a giant mammary gland; now, in his final appearance, Kepesh again identifies as a breast -- more specifically, he identifies himself almost exclusively through his desire, and that desire is for Consuela's ample bust: by its vigor and volume Kepesh asserts his own vitality, and later, only by its corporeal rot, will he despairingly acknowledge the mortality that he has tried to deny. That Kepesh should only feel such impermanence when cancer threatens to rob Consuela of the endowment by which he has been so thoroughly beguiled (and willingly defined), that he should furthermore recast her suffering so callously as his own is a fittingly bathetic punchline for such a world-class narcissist, pedant, and sensualist.<br /><br />Kepesh's onanism is less literal than Portnoy's, but it is even more self-directed. As the dying animal, he is both poignant and pathetic, and Roth renders his despair exquisitely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17216991274770301910" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1967180981" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1967180981" />
    <a title="Lukasz Pruski" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2989615-lukasz-pruski"><img alt="Lukasz Pruski" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362764033p2/2989615.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1967180981?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lukasz Pruski" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lukasz Pruski" href="/user/show/2989615-lukasz-pruski">Lukasz Pruski</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1967180981" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5286657443469853317">"<i>"The transition from thinking of someone in the way you've always thought of that person [...] to whatever signifies to you [...] that the person is close to death, is dying, I experienced at that moment not only as a shock but as a betrayal.</i>"<br/><br/>Philip Roth's <i>The Dying Animal</i> (2001) closes the David Kepesh trilogy that the author began with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1000390956' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>The Breast</i>
</a> and continued with <i>The Professor of Desire</i>, a book that I have not read and now doubt that I ever will. <br/><br/>The book under review, ostensibly about </span>
  <span id="freeText5286657443469853317" style="display:none">"<i>"The transition from thinking of someone in the way you've always thought of that person [...] to whatever signifies to you [...] that the person is close to death, is dying, I experienced at that moment not only as a shock but as a betrayal.</i>"<br /><br />Philip Roth's <i>The Dying Animal</i> (2001) closes the David Kepesh trilogy that the author began with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1000390956" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Breast</i>
</a> and continued with <i>The Professor of Desire</i>, a book that I have not read and now doubt that I ever will. <br /><br />The book under review, ostensibly about the human animal dying, revolves around three deaths: one - George's - is shown in its entire horrifying detail. Two other deaths, Consuela's and Kepesh's own, are still in progress. Consuela reaches to sexually colored acts to qualm her fear of dying. Kepesh seems to be clinging to life mainly through indulging in sex. Even the graphic description of George's death is "enriched" by sexual motifs. I suspect that Mr. Roth's goal is to show the eternal dance of Thanatos and Eros and illustrate his thesis that "[s]ex is [...] the revenge on death." But then why all the juicy bits? Why the details of physiology, like the fascination with bodily fluids, and why the mechanics, like describing the hand moving a particular way? Why do I feel soiled when I read a book by Mr. Roth, despite the obvious depth of his writing when he occasionally focuses on something other than sex?<br /><br />While <i>The Dying Animal</i> deals with crucial questions of human existence and contains truly outstanding passages of prose its main focus and the underpinning of the entire literary structure is the study of professor Kepesh's sexual urges and the ways in which he achieves gratification. To make it clear, there is nothing wrong with portraying characters overwhelmed by sexual obsessions: Mr. Roth himself did it in a masterful way in his extraordinary <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1298401196" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Portnoy's Complaint</i>
</a>. Yet the intensely exhibitionistic way that the author writes about the "chaos of eros" and the inclusion of salacious details make it clear to me that he just plain likes writing about sexual behaviors. The reader is entitled to wonder: maybe Mr. Roth achieves gratification by exposing his private erotic thoughts to the world?<br /><br />Some readers will likely find offensive the combination of male erection and breast cancer, accompanied by Schubert's <i>Death and the Maiden</i> quintet. I find it pathetic instead. References to menstruation are just pretentious and immature. It is symptomatic that for me the highpoint of the novel was the mention of Velásquez' <i>Las Meninas</i> because it reminded me of a true literary masterpiece I have recently read - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1906479489" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>The Roads to Santiago.</i>
</a> <br /><br />Philip Roth may be a great writer, but this compulsion to exhibit his personal obsessions turns me off. It's like Facebook, minus the cat pictures.<br /><br />Two stars.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5286657443469853317" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mqVNyzb8UH5tH/JR6khblbAkaO4o9pFwrgj4sz1GvdvraDIWvsEn+tTMP34wnAx6t9hV5rxrfpFOveG6P6TnjA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kFOGJzWp1iGg1jJE5V9VGrKZYPBH8xUi/VH+rLxKNiPhnvn6vZShpRkF/2s/iwL1tWVd+NNu+sMd5OelvqhsdA==')})
  }

Così rimarremo uniti per sempre. Si lasceranno, naturalmente, poco dopo. Ma una relazione finita è spesso la miccia per quella successiva, soprattutto per chi ha bisogno di conferme. Così, quando Pietro incontra Nadia, s\'innamora all\'istante della sua ritrosia, della sua morbidezza dopo tanti spigoli. Pochi giorni prima delle nozze, però, Teresa magicamente ricompare. E con lei l\'ombra di quello che si sono confessati a vicenda, quasi un avvertimento: «Attento a te». Da quel momento in poi la confidenza che si sono scambiati lo seguirà minacciosa: la buona volontà poggia sulla cattiva coscienza, e Pietro non potrà mai più dimenticarlo. Anche perché Teresa si riaffaccia sempre, puntualmente, davanti a ogni bivio esistenziale. O è lui che continua a cercarla? Dopo il successo internazionale di Lacci e Scherzetto, Domenico Starnone aggiunge una pagina potente al suo lavoro di scavo sull\'ambivalenza delle persone e delle relazioni. Con uno sguardo insieme complice e distaccato, e la leggerezza lancinante che possiedono soltanto le grandi narrazioni, ci racconta di un uomo inadeguato a se stesso e alle proprie ambizioni. Ma in realtà ci racconta di noi, di quanto sismico sia il terreno su cui si regge la costruzione della nostra identità.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"9320382966504967625\" href=\"#\" Ma Simone ha otto anni e nessuno ha chiesto il suo parere.\nAndrea cerca dappertutto una storia che assomigli alla sua, senza trovarla: eppure era convinto che la mitologia fosse l\'archetipo di ogni cosa. Certo, se l\'umanità intera ha il terrore di morire, deve prendere atto che per lui è diventato impossibile. Come può smettere di battere un cuore che non c\'è piú?\n\nChiara Valerio racconta con una leggerezza rara le metamorfosi delle relazioni e la loro meccanica involontaria. Vivi il momento presente e non farti travolgere dalle distrazioni. Guarda il mondo intorno a te come se fosse la prima volta. Ascolta la natura, asseconda le stagioni. Obiettivi a cui tutti aspiriamo, certo, ma che non sappiamo mai come raggiungere. Non lo sapeva nemmeno Morishita Noriko quando, ventenne, cominciò a frequentare le lezioni della signora Takeda per eseguire la cerimonia del tè. Né sapeva che quelle prime lezioni erano l\'inizio di un cammino che sarebbe durato tutta la vita. Ogni giorno è un buon giorno è il racconto di una tradizione antichissima, dei suoi rituali, della sua filosofia piú profonda e delle gioie che può regalare. A tutti noi.\n\n«Ci sono cose che puoi provarci quanto e come vuoi ma non le capisci finché non arriva il momento giusto. Però quando poi un giorno le capisci, dopo non puoi far finta di niente». La cerimonia del tè è uno dei riti tradizionali piú affascinanti del Giappone. I monaci buddisti del sedicesimo secolo hanno codificato ogni passaggio di questo rituale che, attraverso i gesti piú semplici, chiama i partecipanti a concentrarsi sulla profonda ricerca di se stessi. Con quella sua ritualità che immutata attraversa i secoli, la cerimonia del tè sembra qualcosa di molto lontano dalla vita di tutti i giorni. Lo sembrava anche a Morishita Noriko quando, studentessa svogliata e indecisa sulla strada da intraprendere, su consiglio della madre prese a frequentare un corso sulla cerimonia del tè. Non sa che quelle prime lezioni sono l\'inizio di un viaggio che durerà tutta la vita. I momenti dedicati alla cerimonia del tè, ai suoi riti, alla meditazione che impone e, contemporaneamente, dischiude diventano momenti per trovare un senso alle prove che la vita mette davanti a Noriko: un matrimonio annullato poche settimane prima della cerimonia, il tentativo di conciliare il lavoro con il privato, un trasferimento oltreoceano… il caos della vita si riconcilia nel tempo concentrato di una tazza di tè.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7107620735780840634\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer2098515014944372266">Philip Milton Roth was an American novelist. He gained early literary fame with the 1959 collection <i>Goodbye, Columbus</i> (winner of 1960's <b>National Book Award</b>), cemented it with his 1969 bestseller <i>Portnoy's Complaint</i>, and has continued to write critically-acclaimed works, many of which feature his fictional alter ego, Nathan Zuckerman. The Zuckerman novels began with <i>The Ghost Writer</i> in 1979, and in</span>
  <span id="freeText2098515014944372266" style="display:none">Philip Milton Roth was an American novelist. He gained early literary fame with the 1959 collection <i>Goodbye, Columbus</i> (winner of 1960's <b>National Book Award</b>), cemented it with his 1969 bestseller <i>Portnoy's Complaint</i>, and has continued to write critically-acclaimed works, many of which feature his fictional alter ego, Nathan Zuckerman. The Zuckerman novels began with <i>The Ghost Writer</i> in 1979, and include <i>American Pastoral</i> (1997) (winner of the <b>Pulitzer Prize</b>). In May 2011, he won the <b>Man Booker International Prize</b> for lifetime achievement in fiction.</span>
